URN:CornellLabOfOrnithology:EBIRD:OBS1299845480	2022-01-13 12:37:59.073692	7973	species	avibase-C481FB24	Steller's Sea-Eagle	Haliaeetus pelagicus				1					United States	US	Massachusetts	US-MA	Bristol	US-MA-005		30			Westcott Island--Cliff Dr private beach, Assonet Bay	L17156075	P	41.7989610	-71.0898550	2021-12-20	10:33:00	obsr34603	S99202120	Stationary	P21	EBIRD	57			25	1	G7562656	1	1	1		Today's saga began for me at 6:58 am when Jonathan Eckerson called to report that he had just seen the Steller's from the Somerset Boat Ramp area. I was ready for this moment, having been up late helping to vet the reports from Sunday and helping to plan for this inevitability of the bird's rediscovery--I knew the four Eckerson brothers, who bird this stretch of river more than anyone and know every nook, cranny, and viewpoint, would refind this bird if it was available to be refound.----I briefly considered whether this was a drop everything, throw home life into turmoil, and jump in the car scenario or whether I should start packing bags, making breakfast, cleaning up after my dog (gross!), packing cold-weather-gear for my four-year-old, and laying the groundwork for a long day to enjoy the eagle while remaining in good graces at home. Despite my disastrous decisions on Bristol County's other bird twitch of 2021 (the Heermann's Gull), I opted for the smart play here and got out the door a bit slower but with the full support of the team at home. I rolled straight into Mallard Point, where the eagle had been sitting in a tall White Pine on the horizon no less than 1.4 mi distant to the NE. It was so distant that when I peeked in Jeremiah's scope upon arrival, it took me 8-10 sec to spot the bird, even though it was silhouetted in one of the pines on the horizon! Even in those views, it was distinctive though, showing broad white shoulders, a dark head, a white tail, and a huge, yellow bill. It was perhaps even farther than my views in South Korea though!----As Min's patience waned, and Min started to wear on the patience of the birders, we departed back to the car for a snack and a reassessment. The bird was not getting any closer...and if it did, the dozens of watchers would alert us. We opted to try to find a better vantage. It took 20 minutes to drive around and when I did, I ended up on the wrong side of the Taunton River still, checking from Bayview Ave. instead of the Stop n' Shop Distribution Center, where it was actually perched. Recalibrating with JRT, I started heading for the SnS zone, even though he had yet to find a vantage that improved significantly on the views from Mallard Point. As I was en route though, texts started popping that it flew north, and I immediately redirected towards Assonet Bay...my best decision of the day. ----I went straight to the area of the Cliff Ave. private beach on Wescott Island (which residents gave permission to use today and in the off season). I arrived and had a good view of the bay and began systematically checking the trees on the far side and scanning the air for flying eagles. By this point, I had spoken with Andy Eckerson who told me the bird had flown low directly up the river towards Assonet Bay, so I felt I was in the right area. But a couple minutes of searching revealed nothing. I knew JRT had been watching it too, so called him as I continued to cruise and scan the trees. He had less of a sense for where it had gone than Andy Eckerson, but just 20-30 sec into speaking to him he said "I see it" and then I heard him fire 10-15 photos. I was freaking out. I had a sense that he was looking towards me, but our views were different. He checked a mpa. Told me where. It was perched on the left edge of Shoves Neck--exactly where I had been scanning. What was wrong with me? Could he have his nearings wrong? Could I? We compared landmarks, although I did not know where he was and he didn't know where I was. I referenced the marsh with red cedars on the left side...but he couldn't see that. His description made no sense to me. I waffled back and forth and we finally sent pins and map screengrabs back and forth. He clarified where it was, I calmed down and did a more careful scan looking through the trees instead of just in the openings, and spotted it quickly right where he had said all along: on the left edge of Shoves Neck, perched high but not too high. Boom. Scope. Amazing. Steller's point blank (almost...Google says 621m or 2038 ft) in Mass. Min stayed in the car for a bit while I enjoyed this bird totally by myself. At one moment I stopped. It was calling. I was getting out of car and heard the yelping call coming from direction of perched eagle and did not recognize it (but did not think it sounded even a little bit like the wimpy Bald Eagle call), but immediately deduced that it was no bird sound that I knew, and was loud, and therefore must be the Steller's. I started recording just in time and looked through the scope just in time to hear it call again--and see it call again as it opened its beak in time with the sound--just as an immature Bald Eagle flew right to left in the field of view. More than any other moment today (which was incredible) this one will live in my memory. A *calling* Steller's Sea-Eagle in Mass.----I reveled in that for a minute or so, and then started stressing about getting others there. Made a few calls, sent a few pins, and then J&J (&J) rolled up. We had amazing scope looks together for another ten minutes, in near silence (though Min became ever more rambunctious, understandably). The eagle called a few more times. I mostly failed to get more recordings. We had a few holy sh&* moments of just reveling in this bird in this place. I chatted to a local landowner to prepare him for the coming onslaught of birder vehicles. And then it arrived. Cars started rolling in. Liam Waters got it, thankfully, since I heard he had just missed it flying off its perch. Others followed fast on its heels. Min was out and scampering as cars rolled in too fast (understandably, but still). It started calling again. We all tried to record it, as more people started chattering. Justin Lawson rolled in and chatted instead of parking while I was trying to record. A vanload with Connecticut Audubon emblazoned on the side rolled in. The bird dropped lower in the tree and was less visible. People were parking on both sides, nearly blocking the street. I got out of there and went to the private beach, where it was again quiet, the bird was easily scopable if a few meters more distant, and Min and Juniper could run out their energy. Then I looked and the huge yellow bill was missing. Had it flown? Yes...flying dead away. I got it in the scope and called it out to J as it passed over the treeline and dropped away. Flying away it was truly massive and showed the broad white shoulders impressively.  It seemed to be headed straight to Shaws Boat Yard/Dighton Rock SP. More people rolled up and came down to us, only to have us say it had just flown and that they should check the other side of the river. Eventually the crowds heeded and relocated, but many only did so when word came that the bird had been actually relocated. No idea why twitchers rarely go look and just wait to go twitch. But so it was.----We piled back in: J&J and I were the first to arrive and last to leave. Another birder asked to follow me. No problem. I left Min's balance bike (thankfully recovered: thanks JPE!). Off to Dighton Creek SP, where there were 80 cars and counting, with new cars arriving every minute for the next 90. Insanity. Perfect viewing. Birding bliss for all who arrived except for those that arrived too late (sorry TBJ). Many referred to it as an amazing reunion for all regional New England birders: hard to imagine a better occasion for this.	***mega; pure insanity. I am no fan of emojis in eBird comments, but the only words here are &#x1f92f;----Epic day: if you had told me yesterday that I'd be getting audio recordings of Steller's Sea-Eagle vocalizing in Dighton, Mass, I am not sure I ever could have believed it...even though many of us have joked about the Maritimes Steller's making its way south to *our* patch, you never really expect it to actually be 40 minutes from *your* house! Especially gratifying day because my fears about an issue with residents, or police, or something else seemed largely unfounded, especially once the bird was found across the river from Dighton Creek SP, with ample parking for many dozens of cars and giving everyone (and their grandmother) the opportunity to observe the bird safely through scopes, which gave excellent looks at a safe distance, with no need to encroach upon the bird for closer looks or regret how distant these looks were. Better than I could have dreamed!----This adult Steller's does seem individually identifiable, from its pattern of white in the coverts and even primaries. There are not lots of flight shots from today, and more legwork is probably required to firmly establish the same bird theory. But on probability (no evidence for more than one adult Steller's outside of Alaska over the past two years) and early checks of feather details on flight shots, this bird seems to check out as the same as seen in Quebec, New Brunswick, Nova Scotia in June-Nov 2021 and Denali Highway in 2020 (Texas in Mar 2021 also presumed same bird, but maybe not probably so). Watch for more analysis of this bird's movements.----Also worth saying: if raptor vagrancy was not embraced in prior decades, perhaps it can be now. Multiple individual raptors have now turned up far from home range and through individually identifiable plumage features, or tags/bands, have been confirmed to be the same birds moving massive distances (e.g., South Padre Island--&gt;Portland Maine for Great Black-Hawk, Zone-tailed from Nova Scotia to Virginia and almost every state/province in between, tagged California Condors roaming to Wyoming, Bearded Vultures wandering all of Europe including the UK, etc.). Raptors seem to just go on walkabout sometimes: it’s in their nature. Ongoing patterns of terrestrial vagrancy in raptors shows great capability of exploratory wandering, so northerly Short-tailed Hawks, Crested Caracaras, and even wayward Gray Hawks and Zone-taileds, are now seemingly routine as these birds get photographed more and correctly identified more. And large raptors can cover a lot of ground. What's next? Lesser Yellow-headed Vulture or White Hawk in the US? Confidence that Red-backed Hawk records are legit? Anyway, count me as a believer that a Steller's that decides to wander, can wander, especially once it makes landfall in mainland western Alaska.----For chasers on 21 Dec, we wish you the best. This bird's departure to the north was a bit unnerving: it could have left the area. On the plus side, it could have done an exploration and will return to its favorite haunts that it has worked for the past week. Photos from 12 Dec and 17 Dec seemed to be exactly at Dighton Rock SP...or nearby. So maybe it really likes the area, as do a dozen+ Bald Eagles. FYI, not a single serious birder knew about this bird before 19 Dec. The birding community jumped on it with vigor and not a single birder was secretive or unethical in how news spread thereafter. On the other hand, the initial news only broke because word leaked out gradually from the Massachusetts Department of Fish & Game. That leak was intentionally slow: the intention from the state agency was to *not* share this bird...and this was apparently partly because of past bad photographer (and birder?) behavior and reputation as other birds, like Snowy and Long-eared Owls. Warranted? Or not? You be the judge.----But once this reached the birding world, a number of people went above and beyond to veri

Adult Steller’s Sea-Eagle. This is no doubt the same bird which flew over the ice-free north and became trapped in the Atlantic, or possibly the same bird seen in Texas, then seen in June in Quebec/New Brunswick. However, more than one resident along the river stated to having seen the bird for consistently for the past couple weeks at least. One in Assonet Bay has had the bird perching in a large oak in their yard frequently while another living just north of Dighton Rock SP said they had seen it perching out on a dead tree in the water for the past week. ----With only the info that it had last been seen Friday the 17th at Shaw Cove Boat Yard, my brothers and I came up with a game plan for the morning. Matt and Joel covered SCBY up to Broad Cove at sunrise while I stood watch at Mallard Point. At 6:50AM the bird appeared at 10 o'clock of Mallard Point on the far side of the river flying down river. Flew a short distance before landing on the far side, not passing 11 o'clock of Mallard Point. Snagged a couple photos before calling several people and getting the word out. Shortly after Matt and Joel showed up the bird flew down and snagged a fish before returning to its perch. However, shortly after it was chased up river by two Bald Eagles and landed back from the river up in a large white pine stand. It continued here for close to an hour and 30 minutes before taking off again and heading northeast towards Assonet Bay. Sure enough, shortly after Marshall Iliff refound it in Assonet Bay in another stand of white pines, directly across from this viewing point 41.799289,-71.091491. It was viewed here for maybe 40 minutes (calling!!) before it again took off, this time heading to Shaw Cove Boat Yard where Matt and Joel while viewing from Dighton Rock State Park refound it almost instantly. Bird proceeded to chill here for the next several hours before being flushed by someone. Flew north and was viewed circling distantly for some time over the power station before finally disappearing. ----Best bet for tomorrow is probably viewing from Mallard Point before sunrise looking for it to leave roost.

Thanks Amy O'Neill for the great eye.  Lucky timing for me.

They split up and the STSE flew in tight circles, gaining altitude steadily and soon began being harassed by a Common Raven.  It then headed directly away from us (= to north) still being harassed by raven  until we lost sight of it in the scope.

This individual is no doubt the same bird that had been seen in Alaska in 2020, and then possibly in Texas in 3/21.  Then it showed up in Quebec, NB, and Nova Scotia from late June through November.  Word came out on 12/19 that the bird had been photographed in this same area along the Taunton River (and in same tree possibly at Dighton Rock SP!?) on 12/12.  Someone re-found it early on 12/20 and Jane noticed the post to CTBirds while we were enjoying coffee in bed.  So she played hooky from work and we tore down there.  After just barely missing it at a couple of spots we finally caught up with it at Dighton Rocks SP.  It hung out in these trees for a couple of hours and over 200 people got to see it.  Sadly, it appears that a photographer may have flushed it (at about 1:10 PM) as scores of birders were still en route.----Other birders present that saw the bird at Dighton Rock SP that I said hi to included at least: S. Paid little attention other more routine species.	First reported in this area this morning but apparently present for several days before being positively identified.  Presumably the same individual seen in QC, NB, and NS.  See https://www.nytimes.com/2021/11/05/science/stellers-sea-eagle.html. This bird was perched with an imm. BAEA across the Taunton River from Dighton Pt SP. when we arrived, Many, many birders (50+) on site.  ----This was an enormous  Haliaetus eagle, fully a third larger than the nearby imm BAEA.  Huge yellow-orange bill, dark, almost black body with big pure white shoulders down to leading edge of inner wing (secondary coverrts), lower back, tail, undertail coverts and "leggings."  In flight, leading edge of wing was prominently white. I noticed that the flight shape and wing flap were distinctively different from BAEA, less "plank-like."  Tail white and wedge-shaped.----Bird was perched when we arrived near BAEA. After 10 +/- minutes, it defecated and I expected immediate lift-off.  But it settled back in for a minute or two and then both BAEA and STSE lifted off  at 12:58, the BAEA only a fraction of a second sooner.  They split up and the STSE flew in tight circles, gaining altitude steadily and soon began being harassed by a Common Raven.  It then headed directly away from us (= to north) still being harassed by raven  until we lost sight of it in the scope.

Only its head and neck were visible. We heard the eagle calling (sometimes raspy, muted 'kek' calls reminiscent of a gull) before we even saw it! At ~11:08am the eagle took off and flew straight WNW and was then quickly re-found over near Shaw's Boat Yard in the West Branch of the Taunton River. ----When perched the eagle's massively thick and orangey-yellow bill was the most obvious feature. In a scope you could make out details on the head such as the eagle's large pale eye set on a black face, with wispy tawny plumes down the eagle's crown and nape. In flight, the distinctive wing shape and wedge-shaped, white tail were clearly seen in addition to the overall blackish coloration and long white patagial patches. ----A photographer named David Ennis discovered this Steller's Sea-Eagle near Shaw's Boat Yard in the West Branch of the Taunton River on Dec 12th. He isn't a birder, so news was slow to spread to the birding community but the photos were verified and the location was nailed down yesterday, Dec 19th. The Eckersons were on the scene early this morning and quickly discovered the bird at 6:45am along the river a little S of the Dighton Rock State Park. The eagle was tracked around the area all morning before it settled into a pine at Shoves Neck (described above).----This is presumably the same adult Steller's Sea-Eagle roaming widely across North America over the last year; first seen in central AK, then southern TX, then around eastern Canada, and has now pushed south into MA. These two articles do a job summarizing the bird's travels and the theory behind all these sightings pertaining to the same bird: ----https://www.birdguides.com/news/stellers-sea-eagle-tracked-across-north-america/?fbclid=IwAR38ctwWfBThOOoLtFX5jJgY_gXKBR6cv4ftWxz6J58qY6Ws4KWGk0hcLUI----https://www.smithsonianmag.com/smart-news/stellers-sea-eagle-dazzles-birders-on-lone-odyssey-180979035/----Article on the Mass sighting: https://www.masslive.com/boston/2021/12/rare-sighting-of-the-stellers-sea-eagle-has-been-spotted-in-massachusetts-for-the-first-time.html?fbclid=IwAR1nox9OlTNub7HoNo83n3U05kf3lcRZ2jvrPdeOM4hfaEEg3ULM3QvSajA

URN:CornellLabOfOrnithology:EBIRD:OBS1299501166	2021-12-30 21:29:14.482638	7973	species	avibase-C481FB24	Steller's Sea-Eagle	Haliaeetus pelagicus				1					United States	US	Massachusetts	US-MA	Bristol	US-MA-005					Mallard Point	L17150946	H	41.7726055	-71.1205563	2021-12-20	06:40:00	obsr532483	S99171146	Stationary	P21	EBIRD	266			1	1	G7571314	1	1	1			**Incredible; brief summary for those looking for more information: ----First got word of this bird's existent through a massbird post by Brian Cassidy the night before. However, more than one resident along the river stated to having seen the bird for consistently for the past couple weeks at least. One in Assonet Bay has had the bird perching in a large oak in their yard frequently while another living just north of Dighton Rock SP said they had seen it perching out on a dead tree in the water for the past week. ----With only the info that it had last been seen Friday the 17th at Shaw Cove Boat Yard, my brothers and I came up with a game plan for the morning. Matt and Joel covered SCBY up to Broad Cove at sunrise while I stood watch at Mallard Point. At 6:50AM the bird appeared at 10 o'clock of Mallard Point on the far side of the river flying down river. Flew a short distance before landing on the far side, not passing 11 o'clock of Mallard Point. Snagged a couple photos before calling several people and getting the word out. Shortly after Matt and Joel showed up the bird flew down and snagged a fish before returning to its perch. However, shortly after it was chased up river by two Bald Eagles and landed back from the river up in a large white pine stand. It continued here for close to an hour and 30 minutes before taking off again and heading northeast towards Assonet Bay. Sure enough, shortly after Marshall Iliff refound it in Assonet Bay in another stand of white pines, directly across from this viewing point 41.799289,-71.091491. It was viewed here for maybe 40 minutes (calling!!) before it again took off, this time heading to Shaw Cove Boat Yard where Matt and Joel while viewing from Dighton Rock State Park refound it almost instantly. Bird proceeded to chill here for the next several hours before being flushed by someone. Flew north and was viewed circling distantly for some time over the power station before finally disappearing. ----Best bet for tomorrow is probably viewing from Mallard Point before sunrise looking for it to leave roost.

URN:CornellLabOfOrnithology:EBIRD:OBS1299655862	2021-12-21 18:17:52.587191	7973	species	avibase-C481FB24	Steller's Sea-Eagle	Haliaeetus pelagicus				1					United States	US	Massachusetts	US-MA	Bristol	US-MA-005		30			1551 Somerset Avenue, Dighton, Massachusetts, US (41.827, -71.121)	L17153234	P	41.8268550	-71.1208769	2021-12-20	13:03:00	obsr803756	S99186347	Stationary	P21	EBIRD	5			2	1		1	1	1			***Absolutely insane!----Enormous eagle with a bright white tail. White coloration extending about halfway up the flanks. Giant, glaring, bright-yellow bill. Otherwise all dark apart from the bright white areas of the forewings. More limited white forewing on the underside than on top. Incredibly striking bird, and hard to mistake for anything else in this plumage!----After getting out of a doctor's appointment I made a b-line for Dighton Rock State Park, hoping to arrive in time to see this bird. When I was about 15 minutes away, I got a text from Skyler Kardell suggesting I come to the other side of the river where they were having better views. I quickly changed the GPS and it didn't change our time much if at all. Then, when I was 4 minutes away (1:01PM), he texted that the bird had flown. I became extremely nervous that it wouldn't be visible when I arrived, but before I was able to acknowledge the idea that I might miss this bird, I saw <a href="https://ebird.org/checklist/S99187865">Will Sweet, Liam Waters, and Amy O'Neill</a> pulled over in a parking lot, all pointing optics to the sky. We quickly pulled into the lot and I ran over to them, immediately seeing the Steller's Sea-Eagle circling high above the road in good sunlight! We celebrated a bit and watched the bird circling for ~5 minutes before it flew off to the North. Just before it disappeared behind the treeline a Common Raven began harassing it. I didn't see the eagle disappear because I'd briefly ran back over to the car, and when I returned it was no longer visible. I called Skyler to inform him that I'd gotten to see the bird and what direction it was flying in. Even though I only saw the bird at a distance for 5 minutes, this was one of the most incredible birds I've chased!----There have been <a href="https://ebird.org/map/stseag?neg=true&env.minX=-137.90145495788295&env.minY=25.271196285689786&env.maxX=-33.83895495788295&env.maxY=59.280852110830864&zh=true&gp=false&ev=Z&mr=1-12&bmo=1&emo=12&yr=range&byr=2021&eyr=2021">several reports of an adult(s) throughout Northeastern Canada this year</a> during the summer, and then again in November. It seems possible/likely that these all account for the same individual but this still remains uncertain as far as I know.----I see no reason to suggest captive provenance for this individual. They have a known history of turning up as vagrants in Alaska especially. "Vagrants recorded up to 3000 km from regular range on Kure and Midway (Hawaiian Is), and wandering birds recorded as far afield as Anadyr, Yakutsk, Beijing and various parts of SW Alaska (seven records, some of them involving long-staying individuals, mostly Pribilofs and Aleutians)." From <a href="https://birdsoftheworld.org/bow/species/stseag/cur/introduction#mig">Birds of the World</a>. See also <a href="https://ebird.org/checklist/S99202120">MJI and JT's comments</a> regarding the Steller's Sea-Eagle in Canada and raptor vagrancy in general.

URN:CornellLabOfOrnithology:EBIRD:OBS1299685260	2021-12-20 19:50:28.706123	7973	species	avibase-C481FB24	Steller's Sea-Eagle	Haliaeetus pelagicus				1					United States	US	Massachusetts	US-MA	Bristol	US-MA-005		30			Dighton Rock SP	L1431929	H	41.8117549	-71.1063090	2021-12-20	11:22:00	obsr538191	S99188857	Traveling	P22	EBIRD	148	1.127		2	1	G7561168	1	1	1			Continuing adult perched in a bare tree across the bay from the state park, just North of Shaw's Boat Yard. Had just flown over and perched here after being seen perched in a pine at the SW corner of Shoves Neck, and viewed from Cliff Drive on Bryants Neck in the East Branch of the Taunton River.----Immediately upon arriving at the state park, we were shown by other birders already present that the eagle was perched among 4 Bald Eagles across the river, and just N of Shaw's Boat Yard. Throughout the time we observed it, the eagles perched nearby and soaring around the general area all slowly drifted off to the NW which then left the Steller's with a single immature Bald for quite awhile until they too flushed at 12:59pm and headed off to the NW. When the Steller's took flight it lazily soared NW-ward for a moment but quickly attracted a Common Raven which relentlessly hounded the eagle and chased it straight away from us heading NW. After the raven had successfully chased the eagle far, far away from us it broke off the eagle, the made at least 6 triumphant barrel-rolls in the air!----A few times the eagle was heard calling (sometimes raspy, muted 'kek' calls reminiscent of a gull). When perched the eagle's massively thick and orangey-yellow bill was the most obvious feature. In a scope you could make out details on the head such as the eagle's large pale eye set on a black face, with wispy tawny plumes down the eagle's crown and nape. A small white spot is present on the eagle's forehead. A thin orange-yellow orbital ridge can be seen above the eagle's eye. In flight, the distinctive wing shape and wedge-shaped, white tail were clearly seen in addition to the overall blackish coloration and long white patagial patches.----A photographer named David Ennis discovered this Steller's Sea-Eagle near Shaw's Boat Yard in the West Branch of the Taunton River on Dec 12th. He isn't a birder, so news was slow to spread to the birding community but the photos were verified and the location was nailed down yesterday, Dec 19th. The Eckersons were on the scene early this morning and quickly discovered the bird at 6:45am along the river a little S of the Dighton Rock State Park.----This is presumably the same adult Steller's Sea-Eagle roaming widely across North America over the last year; first seen in central AK, then southern TX, then around eastern Canada, and has now pushed south into MA. These two articles do a job summarizing the bird's travels and the theory behind all these sightings pertaining to the same bird:----https://www.birdguides.com/news/stellers-sea-eagle-tracked-across-north-america/?fbclid=IwAR38ctwWfBThOOoLtFX5jJgY_gXKBR6cv4ftWxz6J58qY6Ws4KWGk0hcLUI----https://www.smithsonianmag.com/smart-news/stellers-sea-eagle-dazzles-birders-on-lone-odyssey-180979035/

URN:CornellLabOfOrnithology:EBIRD:OBS1299657260	2021-12-20 20:58:30.569255	7973	species	avibase-C481FB24	Steller's Sea-Eagle	Haliaeetus pelagicus				1					United States	US	Massachusetts	US-MA	Bristol	US-MA-005		30			Shaw's Boat Yard (permission required)	L17156966	P	41.8159802	-71.1149684	2021-12-20	11:44:00	obsr501206	S99186524	Stationary	P21	EBIRD	86			1	1		1	1	1			*CMF*, lifer, and an incredible continuation of what must certainly be the standout rare bird of 2021. Yesterday (12/19) a report surfaced of a STSE in Dighton, MA back on the 12th - apparently the observer was not sure of what they had seen, and the photos took several days to be confirmed. Incredibly the bird was refound this morning (12/20) in the same area, and word spread quickly throughout New England. I followed the bird's progress while driving up, and arrived at Shaw's boat dock just after hearing that the STSE was being seen across the river from Dighton SP. We confirmed with employees of the dock that it would be alright to head to the pier to check for the eagle, and were able to spot it in a tree with two immature BAEA. The STSE remained perched in the tree for an hour after we first spotted it, and seemed very relaxed (preening, sunning, and scanning the area for prey) despite the ever-growing throngs of birders on both sides of the river. At about 1:15PM the STSE took off, heading slightly north to where the Taunton River narrows, and began soaring, before being harassed by a raven and heading west over land, where we lost sight of it. ----To say this bird was unmistakable would be an understatement: nearly double the size of the nearby BAEA, with a clearly wedge-shaped white tail, prominent white patches on the shoulders, and most notably an absolutely colossal, bright yellow bill, like one of those prehistoric Terror Birds, which was visible with the naked eye from over 100 meters. In flight the strongly wedged tail was clearly visible, as well as the huge, broad, paddle-shaped wings, accentuated by the clean white extending up to the rear flanks from the tail & rump. This is a species I've dreamed of seeing since first looking through my old Nat Geo guide, and it did not disappoint. What an absolute behemoth!----The consensus at this time seems to be that this STSE is likely the same individual that was seen sporadically throughout the summer in northern New Brunswick and the Gaspé Peninsula, QC, which was apparently confirmed as the same individual seen in summer of 2020 in interior Alaska, supporting a wild origin from far eastern Russia. A sighting on the 3rd and 4th of November in central Nova Scotia is also thought to be this individual, and this sighting would support a southward trajectory through the late fall into winter consistent with the same individual's appearance in MA. Whether this bird is the same individual seen in AK/QC/NB/NS, or the very enigmatic TX bird remains to be seen, but I (and I'm sure many others) will be keen to learn more about this incredible bird's history - and its next destination!----Any birders checking this location in the future should be aware that this dock is private property, and permission MUST be granted from the employees before using the docks to search the riverbanks. They are aware of the situation regarding the eagle, but regardless please check before entering!

URN:CornellLabOfOrnithology:EBIRD:OBS1300958434	2021-12-30 21:29:14.482638	7973	species	avibase-C481FB24	Steller's Sea-Eagle	Haliaeetus pelagicus				1					United States	US	Massachusetts	US-MA	Bristol	US-MA-005					Mallard Point	L17150946	H	41.7726055	-71.1205563	2021-12-20	06:40:00	obsr508253	S99284132	Stationary	P21	EBIRD	266			1	1	G7571314	1	1	1			**Incredible; brief summary for those looking for more information: ----First got word of this bird's existent through a massbird post by Brian Cassidy the night before. However, more than one resident along the river stated to having seen the bird for consistently for the past couple weeks at least. One in Assonet Bay has had the bird perching in a large oak in their yard frequently while another living just north of Dighton Rock SP said they had seen it perching out on a dead tree in the water for the past week. ----With only the info that it had last been seen Friday the 17th at Shaw Cove Boat Yard, my brothers and I came up with a game plan for the morning. Matt and Joel covered SCBY up to Broad Cove at sunrise while I stood watch at Mallard Point. At 6:50AM the bird appeared at 10 o'clock of Mallard Point on the far side of the river flying down river. Flew a short distance before landing on the far side, not passing 11 o'clock of Mallard Point. Snagged a couple photos before calling several people and getting the word out. Shortly after Matt and Joel showed up the bird flew down and snagged a fish before returning to its perch. However, shortly after it was chased up river by two Bald Eagles and landed back from the river up in a large white pine stand. It continued here for close to an hour and 30 minutes before taking off again and heading northeast towards Assonet Bay. Sure enough, shortly after Marshall Iliff refound it in Assonet Bay in another stand of white pines, directly across from this viewing point 41.799289,-71.091491. It was viewed here for maybe 40 minutes (calling!!) before it again took off, this time heading to Shaw Cove Boat Yard where Matt and Joel while viewing from Dighton Rock State Park refound it almost instantly. Bird proceeded to chill here for the next several hours before being flushed by someone. Flew north and was viewed circling distantly for some time over the power station before finally disappearing. ----Best bet for tomorrow is probably viewing from Mallard Point before sunrise looking for it to leave roost.

When perched the eagle's massively thick and orangey-yellow bill was the most obvious feature. In a scope you could make out details on the head such as the eagle's large pale eye set on a black face, with wispy tawny plumes down the eagle's crown and nape. A small white spot is present on the eagle's forehead. A thin orange-yellow orbital ridge can be seen above the eagle's eye. In flight, the distinctive wing shape and wedge-shaped, white tail were clearly seen in addition to the overall blackish coloration and long white patagial patches. ----A photographer named David Ennis discovered this Steller's Sea-Eagle near Shaw's Boat Yard in the West Branch of the Taunton River on Dec 12th. He isn't a birder, so news was slow to spread to the birding community but the photos were verified and the location was nailed down yesterday, Dec 19th. The Eckersons were on the scene early this morning and quickly discovered the bird at 6:45am along the river a little S of the Dighton Rock State Park.----This is presumably the same adult Steller's Sea-Eagle roaming widely across North America over the last year; first seen in central AK, then southern TX, then around eastern Canada, and has now pushed south into MA. These two articles do a job summarizing the bird's travels and the theory behind all these sightings pertaining to the same bird: ----https://www.birdguides.com/news/stellers-sea-eagle-tracked-across-north-america/?fbclid=IwAR38ctwWfBThOOoLtFX5jJgY_gXKBR6cv4ftWxz6J58qY6Ws4KWGk0hcLUI----https://www.smithsonianmag.com/smart-news/stellers-sea-eagle-dazzles-birders-on-lone-odyssey-180979035/----Article on the Mass sighting: https://www.masslive.com/boston/2021/12/rare-sighting-of-the-stellers-sea-eagle-has-been-spotted-in-massachusetts-for-the-first-time.html?fbclid=IwAR1nox9OlTNub7HoNo83n3U05kf3lcRZ2jvrPdeOM4hfaEEg3ULM3QvSajA

Paid little attention other more routine species.	First reported in this area this morning but apparently present for several days before being positively identified.  Presumably the same individual seen in QC, NB, and NS.  See https://www.nytimes.com/2021/11/05/science/stellers-sea-eagle.html. This bird was perched with an imm. BAEA across the Taunton River from Dighton Pt SP. when we arrived, Many, many birders (50+) on site.  ----This was an enormous  Haliaetus eagle, fully a third larger than the nearby imm BAEA.  Huge yellow-orange bill, dark, almost black body with big pure white shoulders down to leading edge of inner wing (secondary coverrts), lower back, tail, undertail coverts and "leggings."  In flight, leading edge of wing was prominently white. I noticed that the flight shape and wing flap were distinctively different from BAEA, less "plank-like."  Tail white and wedge-shaped.----Bird was perched when we arrived near BAEA. After 10 +/- minutes, it defecated and I expected immediate lift-off.  But it settled back in for a minute or two and then both BAEA and STSE lifted off  at 12:58, the BAEA only a fraction of a second sooner.  They split up and the STSE flew in tight circles, gaining altitude steadily and soon began being harassed by a Common Raven.  It then headed directly away from us (= to north) still being harassed by raven  until we lost sight of it in the scope.

What an incredible bird and an incredible experience! Sooo cold though. Had to take lots of breaks to warm up in the car.	Continuing MEGA MEGA!!! Wow such an incredible bird!!!!!!!! Started morning search at Oak Point Farm a few minuets past 7am near where the bird had been last sighted the night before. Didn't find any eagles there but after spending about 15 minuets there another birder, Karen, arrived and we continued the search. At about 7:30 am Karen received coordinates of a location where the STSE had been sighted, right across from the Boothbay Harbor Post Office. We were about a 4 minuet drive from there so we immediately headed to that location. When I arrived at the Post Office there were already many birders present but still a few parking spaces left in the parking lot. As soon as I got out of my vehicle I spotted the STSE perched in a pine at the edge of the frozen inlet. Massive dark brown eagle with white wedge shaped tail, white shoulder patches, almost comically large bright yellow bill, and yellow feet. I observed the bird from about 7:30am until 11:41am when it finally took flight. Thank you to everyone for sharing all the helpful information that made it possible to see this bird! It was Brutally cold, about 0ºF at 7am "warming up" to about 11º F for a high, also about 20mph winds made it feel even colder!!! Had to take multiple breaks in the car to warm up or thaw out! But so worth it! Waited until bird flew off at around 11:41am. Lifer!

Snow cover: the ground is mostly bare, except for scattered patches of ice.--Despite not having seen the sea eagle fly north up Townsend Gut yesterday evening, we still suspected that it was roosting somewhere to the north, and so decided it was worth a check at dawn. And indeed it was! This morning there were many more observers than yesterday morning, although still perhaps only a dozen or so, and some of these were not quick enough to spot the sea eagle. In hindsight, we have to wonder if yesterday it hadn't flown up the Gut earlier than expected - perhaps even at noon? - which would account for the lack of sightings all afternoon, and the fact that we had missed it here at dusk last night.--Sunday. Clear blue skies and bright sun, still cold with light winds	Second encounter of the day with this incredible wandering individual, initial sighting details included in this checklist: https://ebird.org/checklist/S100400825 . After watching the eagle fly east across Boothbay Harbor from its perch on Juniper Point, we drove back to the Maine State Aquarium in the hopes of getting better views. The bird was soon refound perched high in a pine on the far shoreline, mostly obscured by the tree’s branches. The bold white wing markings and heavy golden beak helped pick it out at this distance. Eventually a pair of ravens began harassing it, appearing as small as crows in comparison to the hulking raptor. The eagle soon took off, initially flying west towards us but subsequently turning to head north along the treeline with the corvids in hot pursuit. A Bald Eagle soon joined the circling mob scene, allowing for another amazing direct size comparison with the Steller’s, who appeared a third as big again. Eventually the sea-eagle broke off from its pursuers and continued north, disappearing behind the trees. As far as I know, there were no confirmed reports for the rest of the day

URN:CornellLabOfOrnithology:EBIRD:OBS1318364636	2022-02-02 13:38:56.663197	7973	species	avibase-C481FB24	Steller's Sea-Eagle	Haliaeetus pelagicus				1					United States	US	Maine	US-ME	Lincoln	US-ME-015		14			40 Beach Road, Boothbay Harbor, Maine, US (43.837, -69.613)	L17429431	P	43.8369191	-69.6131843	2022-01-14	10:36:00	obsr182152	S100726935	Stationary	P21	EBIRD	22			1	1		1	1	1		36F, cloudy, W 5MPH--pin at the eagle location	continuing stakeout, --very large dark brown eagle with large white shoulder patches (lesser and median upper-wing coverts).  White tail, very large, yellow bill.  not seen in flight.----Based on reports from this past week, it appears the bird has an area of about 14 square kilometers) - area from Townsend Gut to Spruce Point peninsula to the north end of Squirrel Island.  Note, however, that satellite tracking of winter range for juvenile Steller's Sea Eagles suggest that they may have 1 - 3 sub-areas that they hunt (most only have one area). The size of these areas were 274 square km for the first, 529 square km for the second, and 1,181 square km for the third (McGrady MJ. Ibis 2003, 145, 318–328)  Thus, this bird is currently likely being seen in a very small area of what could be its "wintering area."  Based on the data from immature birds, the bird's winter range could extend another 5-10 miles east and/or west from Boothbay Harbor.  ----For those looking, I think the best spot is to start early (6:45am) at Townsend Gut.  Then, to scope either from the aquarium or the end of Rock Road near the aquarium (park at the aquarium and walk down). The Rock Road spot allows you to scan the entire southern bay and Mouse Island (where it has appeared the several afternoons before it goes to roost in Townsend Gut).  Obviously, you should subscribe to get alerts via the GroupMe chat for the Steller's Sea Eagle chase group.

URN:CornellLabOfOrnithology:EBIRD:OBS1316705163	2022-01-17 18:11:31.714043	7973	species	avibase-C481FB24	Steller's Sea-Eagle	Haliaeetus pelagicus				1					United States	US	Maine	US-ME	Lincoln	US-ME-015					McKown Point, Boothbay Harbor	L492164	H	43.8435887	-69.6397483	2022-01-12	07:20:00	obsr633952	S100588824	Traveling	P22	EBIRD_ME	80	0.805		1	1		1	1	1			YES! Fourth time’s the charm—had my lucky swallow earrings which i think did it. Put on a show flying around the whole harbor from its original perch on Spruce Pt, landed on a pine visible from the aquarium wharf. Mega-sized MEGA the most notable field mark! Seemed perfectly at ease coasting into the bitter snowy southwest wind while the gulls struggled around it; the smoothness of its flight and the stark white of the lesser coverts and retrices made it appear almost more kite than bird, especially on a glide when the arrow-shaped tail was apparent. Local long-tails scurried off as it banked in, and it seemed to be eying the less nervous mergs as it perched for the next half hour. Memorable views from there of the huge cartoonish orange bill, chicken-splayed powerful yellow talons, and even the keen yellow eye. Buffeted by occasional gusts but weathered them with little more than a ruffling of feathers; at one point bent to half-heartedly preen its breast feathers. Still roosting when I left, in numerous scope views.

URN:CornellLabOfOrnithology:EBIRD:OBS1317851880	2022-02-04 14:13:19.667925	7973	species	avibase-C481FB24	Steller's Sea-Eagle	Haliaeetus pelagicus				1					United States	US	Maine	US-ME	Lincoln	US-ME-015					23 Factory Cove Rd, Boothbay Harbor US-ME 43.83739, -69.62880	L17417792	P	43.8373910	-69.6287950	2022-01-13	11:05:00	obsr6553	S100685537	Stationary	P21	EBIRD_PA	61			2	1	G7710058	1	1	1		There were many, many other people here besides Mike & I.	Continuing MEGA-rarity. Large dark brown bird with white shoulder patch and huge orange-yellow bill. After the bird flew overhead at the aquarium (see list https://ebird.org/pa/checklist/S100876866) Mike & I went in the direction it had flown to try to locate it. We stopped at Bristol Lobster Sales but the vantage point was not good to the south. So we drove further to the next road that went out to the west which was Factory Cove Road. Towards the end of that road there was a car pulled over and as we pulled up to it we saw the eagle sitting in the top of the pine tree we had seen it land in when we were at the aquarium. I put the location out on the GroupMe thread and gradually many more people arrived to view this bird which sat here for more than an hour. I stopped this list well before the bird left which was about 12:45 PM. I was happy to see that everyone was very respectful of private property and careful not to do anything to flush the bird. Some of the residents came out of their houses and asked what was happening and were shown the eagle through various birders' scopes.

BOOTHBAY HARBOR AREA DAY 3:  After not finding the sea-eagle on the 9th and 10th a late morning report came out that the Steller’s was seen prior to 9:00 this morning in the Birch Road area on the eastern shore of the Sprucewold/Spruce Point peninsula southeast of Boothbay Harbor.  Many chasers then quickly made it over to Barrett Park to try to spot the bird from the park.--- - ---I met a local resident there who was very familiar with the Birch Road neighborhood, and who volunteered to lead us to look for the bird there and then (unbeknownst to us ahead of time) eventually to a nearby private property vantage point off of Birch Road.  Amazingly (and unbeknownst to him ahead of time), upon arriving we immediately observed the sea-eagle preening for about 10 minutes almost heavily obscured in a large evergreen tree above the shoreline there!--- - ---The Steller’s shortly launched from its perch, flying east over the water, and continued to about a quarter of a mile out over Linekin Bay, then it turned south powerfully chasing a large bird in flight and quickly disappeared to the south around the headland continuing at least well into south Linekin Bay.  We tried to relocate the Steller’s in the Spruce Point area but could not. --- - ---The sea-eagle had been extremely elusive for almost everyone searching for the bird the two days prior.  DSLR photos and handheld phonescoped video footage were obtained (through a bit of shimmer) by these two extremely happy sea-eagle chasers from very far away!--- - -   - - -   - - ---- - -   - - -   - - ---1/19 2022 UPDATE AND AMAZING TWIST TO THE SAGA:--On the 18th, 3 days after arriving safely back in Georgia from Maine, I was browsing through YouTube footage of Steller’s Sea-Eagles and came upon this:--https://m.youtube.com/watch?v=SaHowv-RJK4--- - ---A CAPTIVE STELLER’S SEA-EAGLE NAMED KODIAK (OR KODY), FROM THE NATIONAL AVIARY IN PITTSBURGH PENNSYLVANIA, HAD ESCAPED ON SEPTEMBER 25TH AND WAS AT LARGE!!!!--- - ---FAIRLY HORRIFIED, I WONDERED IF HUNDREDS OR MAYBE EVEN THOUSANDS OF BIRDERS (ME INCLUDED) WERE ALL MERELY CHASING AN ESCAPED STELLER’S SEA-EAGLE IN NEW ENGLAND???!!!--- - ---After much further searching I discovered, with immense relief, that Kodiak had remained in Pittsburgh and was retrieved there by aviary staff on October 3rd, 2021:--https://m.youtube.com/watch?v=axGiFepPd18--Here is the National Aviary’s page concerning the recovery of Kodiak on October 3rd, 2021:--https://www.aviary.org/birds-habitats/kodiak-the-stellers-sea-eagle/--- - ---BUT, I still needed to know if Kody was in captivity when I had seen the supposedly wild bird in Maine, just for my peace of mind, so I contacted the National Aviary and submitted a request for a Kody status check!--- - ---Concerning Kodiak’s whereabouts since being recovered in October the National Aviary responded on 1/19 2022 as follows:----Hi Mark,--Yes, indeed, our bird is securely in our care, and has been all winter.  The coincidence of a wild Steller's here in the East is quite remarkable, though!--Best,--Bob--Robert S. Mulvihill--Ornithologist--National Aviary--700 Arch Street--Pittsburgh, PA 15212--Main: 412-323-7235--Direct: 412-522-5729--Email: robert.mulvihill@aviary.org--- - -   - - -   - - ---- - -   - - -   - - ---Further checking of all eBird Steller's Sea-Eagle accepted checklists to date for the lower 48 United States and eastern North America reveals confirmed sighting timeframes in Eastern North America as follows (and interestingly no sightings of our wild roaming Steller's were confirmed in September or October 2021 during Kodiak's brief days of freedom in Pittsburgh):----2021 3/7 - Goliad County Texas United States--2021 6/29-7/23 - Restigouche River area (Tide Head), both Avignon County Quebec Canada and Restigouche County New Brunswick Canada--2021 7/7-31 - La Côte-de-Gaspé County area (Gaspe), Quebec Canada--2021 8/7-8 - La Matanie County area (Matane), Quebec Canada--2021 11/3-4 - Windsor/Falmouth/Newport Landi

Stationary watch at both Ft Wiliam Henry and Pemaquid Seafood Dock (private).	Yes!!  After searching unsuccessfully all morning (5 hours), the STSE was finally spotted just before noon and we came back to Ft William Henry to see it. It was perched near the top of a tall tree about 0.75 mile away from us, north of Pemaquid Seafood's dock on the point directly opposite of Fossetts Cove. It stayed perched there for a long time, so we moved to Pemaquid Seafood's dock to be closer.  From this location, it was about 0.3-0.4 mile away (still within same eBird location/same hotspot).  After another long while, it left this perch, and to everyone's surprise (about 50 people were at same location), it flew along the shoreline directly toward us, and passed by us giving an epic fly-by flight view! Unbelievably lucky, this was one of the most amazing bird sights I've ever seen!

Snow cover: the ground is mostly bare, except for scattered patches of ice.--Word went out on the rare bird alert that the sea eagle was perched on Pooler Road, so we raced to figure out where in the heck Pooler Road was, and then how to get there. Of course, everyone one of the many eagle hunters in the area had the same inclination, so the road quickly became jammed with cars and birders. We pulled into the first quasi-close parking spot that we found, and then, following the crowd, rushed down the road to the site.--Sunday. Civil twilight begins 06:37, sunrise 07:08; sunset 16:28, civil twilight ends 16:59. Eastern Standard Time.--Mammals: none observed.	continuing mega rarity. perched in a pine at the end of Pooler Road. flew off shortly after we arrived, and almost immediately was lost from view. all of us had some kind of a view of it, although Jay, who got here first, probably saw more of it than Shawn or I did. for my part, my eye glasses were quite fogged from rushing along, and I didn't have the presence of mind to take them off in time: my only view of the eagle was through the fogged-over glasses, just as it took flight and then disappeared from sight. not a great end to my sea eagle experience, but given all that had come before, I had nothing to complain about.

Reasoning that we’d have the best odds of spotting it looking north, we went searching for a public vantage point and found Pine Cliff Road. Our instincts turned out to be spot on, and we quickly found the bird on its prominent perch. Several other birders who had the same idea were gathered a little further up the road, leading to much rejoicing and several lovely reunions. Sunny and bright, but still quite breezy and chilly	Incredible record, long awaited chase opportunity after watching the saga develop for months. Finally tracked down after a sunrise sighting of the bird flying south reported from the boat launch between West Boothbay Harbor and Southport. Perched in a tall pine on Juniper Point, scoped looking north from Pine Cliff Road. Massive and imposing raptor, dark brown overall with brilliant white shoulder patches and tail. The immense golden bill was a striking field mark even at a distance. Constantly scanning the harbor, intensity of its glare was palpable whenever it looked our way. A goodly number of birders were able to observe it from this site until a group of observers approached on foot, walking through the intertidal zone right up to the base of its tree. At this point, it took off and headed across Boothbay Harbor, low over the water, coming to roost on the far eastern shore. We subsequently headed back over to the Maine State Aquarium to try our luck from that vantage point, details included in this checklist: https://ebird.org/checklist/S100401072

In flight, observed prominent white shoulders.  White area of forehead was observed. Orangish yellow feet. Large yellow/orangish bill and cere. White tail with white extending to flank area. Wedge appearance of tail evident. Primaries distinct, large and somewhat separated during flight  No antagonistic behavior observed during flight, but through personal communication with other observers, there was evidence of possible hunting of Canada Goose. In flight STSE came in over harbor from east (Fossetts Cove) flying at low altitude (approximately 15 feet above pier and dock associated with the Contented Sole building on north shore of Colonial Pemaquid State Historic Site;  43.879966, -69.521561) and proceeded west to tree and perched.  During perch, feathers around supraorbital ridge and head were fluffed. Head not extended but relaxed and neck feathers extended exhibiting warming behavior and actions.  Belly and flank feathers were extended over feet.  Individual appeared to be in relaxed mode and not agitated.  Some preening occurred before extension of body feathers.  STSE would periodically scan area below and outward from perch. Approximately at 16:14 STSE extended neck and was scanning area below perch, then at 16:21 STSE left perch and flew eastward, gathered elevation and turned back to west and flew over Thurston Point.  Anecdotally presumed to fly back toward the Boothbay Harbor area that it was seen previously.  STSE adult seemed to be in good health, non-agitated behavior, although prior to leaving perch seemed to be alerted to disturbance or possible prey.  American Crows were seen to mob the STSE adult while in the area before observed at perch (personal communication with other observers), and this possibly was the reason for movement outside of other perch sites in the area, although as stated earlier, some possible predation attempts were performed before my observations.

URN:CornellLabOfOrnithology:EBIRD:OBS1319384730	2022-01-20 21:59:52.258041	7973	species	avibase-C481FB24	Steller's Sea-Eagle	Haliaeetus pelagicus				1					United States	US	Maine	US-ME	Lincoln	US-ME-015					Boothbay Harbor Yacht Club	L17437703	P	43.8475516	-69.6454013	2022-01-15	07:55:00	obsr108846	S100812337	Traveling	P22	EBIRD	234	0.274		3	1	G7720688	1	1	1		Brutally cold. Clear (visibility 10 miles); wind variable at 7 mph to NW at 10 mph, gusting to 22 mph; 1-7º F. Snow cover: the ground is mostly bare, except for scattered patches of ice. West Harbor Pond is completely frozen over, but the water below the bridge in Boothbay Harbor remains open.--We raced over to the Boothbay Harbor Yacht Club, stopped in their parking lot, and raced west across the road, where the sea eagle was quite obvious. A large crowd quickly assembled; unexpectedly, but not really surprisingly, Ann Mitchell was there too. For the first few minutes we were content to view from the initial location, but, realizing that a side view might be better (even if slightly more distant), we slipped down to the bridge across the mouth of West Harbor Pond. Only a few other observers were there when we arrived, but many others later joined us, some parking on the side of the road, or even on the bridge. Not surprisingly, this became an issue in short order, and after a couple of hours a few police arrived to try to keep both cars and observers in line. (It ws impressive to see, though, how quickly the crowds cleared after the eagle left.) We really wanted to see the bird in flight, but it persisted in giving us absolutely crippling views of it perched, for hour after hour; so periodically we'd take our chances and go sit in the car for a while to warm up, before heading back out for another stint for forced sea eagle viewing.--Note that this site is just north of the aquarium, across the harbor. We gather that Ross Gallardy and his party had a quick sighting of this bird early in the morning (from the Southport Swing Bridge?), which they did not post, but which sent them on a quest for the eagle. And after a thankfully short search, scanning all close coves and inlets, they came across the bird here. Congrats, and many thanks!--Saturday. Civil twilight begins 06:37, sunrise 07:09; sunset 16:26, civil twilight ends 16:58. Eastern Standard Time.--Mammals: none observed.	continuing mega rarity. extraordinarily cooperative, almost too much so. when we screamed into the parking lot here and got out of the car, it was perched in full sun in a tall pine on the west side of West Harbor Pond, across from the yacht club, and not 100 yards away. the massive eagle was facing us, which allowed for great view of the overall bulk, the massive orangey yellow bill, the beady yellow iris, the details of the shaggy brown head and breast plumage, and the white, graduated tail; but could see only a small piece of the white shoulders (and of only one shoulder to boot). after a while, then, we drifted down to the bridge below, where we could view it from more of an angle, and get a somewhat better appreciation of it. the eagle was quite aware of its surroundings, looking around constantly, but otherwise not doing much: it was big news if it do much as pulled one foot up and tucked it into the plumage. every hour or so it would act as if it might fly: pooping and wiggling the tail; or leaning forward and raising the tail (again pooping?), in each case leading to speculation that it was about to fly. and we all wanted it to fly, we wanted to see the majesty of those broad white shoulders and get a better sense of the overall heft of the bird. despite the apparent flight intention actions, and despite that fact that we were slowly dying in the brutal cold, however, the eagle stubbornly would not take flight. rarely if ever have I waited so long or suffered so much to see a rare bird in flight. after almost four hours, it finally did something dramatic, and turned around on its perch. this presented a possibility that we had not even counted upon: that it might take flight, but instead of flying right past or even over us, it might fly directly away. and that's about what it did: it soon took flight, heading away, and would have been lost behind the trees had it not angled off to the north and up a bit, so we had a decent view of it flight, albeit trending in the wrong direction. just at the end, it turned west again, and we lost it behind trees. as best that we can determine, it was not seen again for the rest of the day. many photos by Jay, Shawn (but none calendar worthy?), and many others.

Snow cover: the ground is mostly bare, except for scattered patches of ice.--Word went out on the rare bird alert that the sea eagle was perched on Pooler Road, so we raced to figure out where in the heck Pooler Road was, and then how to get there. Of course, everyone one of the many eagle hunters in the area had the same inclination, so the road quickly became jammed with cars and birders. We pulled into the first quasi-close parking spot that we found, and then, following the crowd, rushed down the road to the site.--Sunday. Clear (visibility 10 miles); calm; -2º F. Snow cover: the ground is mostly bare, except for scattered patches of ice.--Despite not having seen the sea eagle fly north up Townsend Gut yesterday evening, we still suspected that it was roosting somewhere to the north, and so decided it was worth a check at dawn. And indeed it was! This morning there were many more observers than yesterday morning, although still perhaps only a dozen or so, and some of these were not quick enough to spot the sea eagle. In hindsight, we have to wonder if yesterday it hadn't flown up the Gut earlier than expected - perhaps even at noon? - which would account for the lack of sightings all afternoon, and the fact that we had missed it here at dusk last night.--Sunday. Civil twilight begins 06:37, sunrise 07:08; sunset 16:28, civil twilight ends 16:59. Eastern Standard Time.--Mammals: none observed.	continuing mega rarity. while my attention was focused on trying to relocate the female Hooded Merganser that we had seen here yesterday evening, we were alerted to the arrival of the sea eagle when someone watching from the east end of the bridge called out at ca 07:05 that it was coming. we snapped to attention, and had a terrific - if all too brief - view as it flew south, fairly low down, over the bridge and farther down the Gut. great views upstream from the bridge, when it was in better light; south of the bridge it was more backlit, and so became more of a silhouette. photographed by Jay, Shawn, and no doubt others.

I was delighted to run into Doug Gochfeld, Michael and Louise, and Tom Johnson.	Third time's a charm!  I arrived at Colonial Pemaquid State Historic Site about 3pm and found that the bird had just been seen flying and then landed on a dock, I believe. I missed it. About 10 min later I was talking to a friend then walked over to a group of folks only to find that it just flew again and went left around the corner and further up the cove out of sight. I missed it again. Many people left. I stayed. About 20 min later I saw it come back around the corner from up the cove. I yelled to get the few remaining people on the bird. It then flew straight at us with the sun behind us and perfect lighting. Flying low it kept coming and flew passed us to the left. I snapped photos constantly as it approach, then my battery died! DIED! Darn! But I had a few shots that I hoped were good enough. The Eagle then landed across the cove and we set our scopes up and got all the newcomers on the bird before it flew again. It stayed for maybe 30 min, then pooped, stretched upward, and flew off along the tree tops to the right, then 180 turn and back to the left and over the trees and disappeared. What an amazing afternoon. In the crowd were folks from Maine, Texas, Oklahoma, Indiana, Alabama, New Jersey, New York, and I bet several more distant places.

It then flew back west and out of sight to neighboring bay.--Huge orange-yellow beak on dark head and body, except white tail and diagnostic broad white shoulders.

URN:CornellLabOfOrnithology:EBIRD:OBS1318948042	2022-02-12 22:44:13.242544	7973	species	avibase-C481FB24	Steller's Sea-Eagle	Haliaeetus pelagicus				1					United States	US	Maine	US-ME	Lincoln	US-ME-015					Boothbay Harbor Yacht Club	L17437703	P	43.8475516	-69.6454013	2022-01-15	07:55:00	obsr135617	S100772827	Traveling	P22	EBIRD	234	0.274		3	1	G7720688	1	1	1		Brutally cold. Clear (visibility 10 miles); wind variable at 7 mph to NW at 10 mph, gusting to 22 mph; 1-7º F. Snow cover: the ground is mostly bare, except for scattered patches of ice. West Harbor Pond is completely frozen over, but the water below the bridge in Boothbay Harbor remains open.--We raced over to the Boothbay Harbor Yacht Club, stopped in their parking lot, and ran west across the road, where the sea eagle was quite obvious. A large crowd quickly assembled; unexpectedly, but not really surprisingly, Ann Mitchell was there too (and apparently so was David Bates, although I did not know this at the time, and did not recognize him if I even saw him). For the first few minutes we were content to view from the initial location, but, realizing that a side view might be better (even if a bit more distant), we slipped down to the bridge across the mouth of West Harbor Pond. Only a few other observers were there when we arrived, but many others later joined us, some parking on the side of the road, or even on the bridge. Not surprisingly, this became a traffic issue in short order, and after a couple of hours a few police arrived to try to keep both cars and observers in line. (It was impressive to see, though, how quickly the crowds cleared after the eagle left.) We really wanted to see the bird in flight, but it persisted in giving us absolutely crippling views of it perched, for hour after hour; so periodically we'd take our chances and go sit in the car for a while to warm up (Jay did a good job of parking the car in spots with good visibility of the sea eagle), before heading back out for another stint for forced sea eagle viewing.--Note that this site is just north of the aquarium, across the harbor. We gather that Ross Gallardy and Zach Millen had a quick sighting of this bird early in the morning (from the Southport Swing Bridge?), which they did not post, but which sent them checking the nearby coves on a quest for the eagle. And after a thankfully short search, scanning all close coves and inlets, they came across the bird here. Congrats, and many thanks!--Saturday. Civil twilight begins 06:37, sunrise 07:09; sunset 16:26, civil twilight ends 16:58. Eastern Standard Time.--Mammals: none observed.	continuing mega rarity. extraordinarily cooperative, almost too much so. when we screamed into the parking lot here and got out of the car, it was perched in full sun in a tall pine on the west side of West Harbor Pond, across from the yacht club, and not 100 yards away. the hefty eagle was facing us, which allowed for great view of the overall bulk, the massive orangey yellow bill, the beady yellow iris, the details of the shaggy brown head and breast plumage, and the white, graduated tail; but we could see only a small piece of the white shoulders (and of only one shoulder to boot). after a while, then, we drifted down to the bridge below, where we could view it from more of an angle, and get a somewhat better appreciation of it. the eagle was quite aware of its surroundings, looking around constantly, but otherwise not doing much: it was big news if it did so much as pull one foot up and tuck it into the plumage. every hour or so it would act as if it might fly: pooping and wiggling the tail; or leaning forward and raising the tail (again pooping?), in each case leading to speculation that it was about to leave. and we all wanted it to fly, we wanted to see the majesty of those broad white shoulders and get a better sense of the overall heft of the bird. despite the apparent flight intention actions, and despite that fact that we were slowly dying in the brutal cold, however, the eagle stubbornly would not take flight. rarely if ever have I waited so long or suffered so much to see a rare bird fly away. after almost four hours, it finally did something dramatic, and turned around on its perch. this presented a possibility that we had not even counted upon: that it might take flight, but instead of flying right past or even over us, it might fly directly away. and that's about what it did: it soon took off, heading away, and would have been lost behind the trees almost immediately had it not angled off to the north and up a bit, so we had a decent, if brief, view of it flight, albeit trending in the wrong direction. just at the end, it turned west again, and we lost it behind trees. ('we' here refers only to Shawn and to me; Jay unfortunately missed this episode, as he had gone back to our Airbnb for an ill-timed bathroom break.) as best that we can determine, it was not seen again for the rest of the day. many photos by Jay, Shawn (but none calendar worthy?), and many others.

Finally tracked down after a sunrise sighting of the bird flying south reported from the boat launch between West Boothbay Harbor and Southport. Perched in a tall pine on Juniper Point, scoped looking north from Pine Cliff Road. Massive and imposing raptor, dark brown overall with brilliant white shoulder patches and tail. The immense golden bill was a striking field mark even at a distance. Constantly scanning the harbor, intensity of its glare was palpable whenever it looked our way. A goodly number of birders were able to observe it from this site until a group of observers approached on foot, walking through the intertidal zone right up to the base of its tree. At this point, it took off and headed across Boothbay Harbor, low over the water, coming to roost on the far eastern shore. We subsequently headed back over to the Maine State Aquarium to try our luck from that vantage point, details included in this checklist: https://ebird.org/checklist/S100401072

Clear blue skies and bright sun, still cold with light winds	Second encounter of the day with this incredible wandering individual, initial sighting details included in this checklist: https://ebird.org/checklist/S100400825 . After watching the eagle fly east across Boothbay Harbor from its perch on Juniper Point, we drove back to the Maine State Aquarium in the hopes of getting better views. The bird was soon refound perched high in a pine on the far shoreline, mostly obscured by the tree’s branches. The bold white wing markings and heavy golden beak helped pick it out at this distance. Eventually a pair of ravens began harassing it, appearing as small as crows in comparison to the hulking raptor. The eagle soon took off, initially flying west towards us but subsequently turning to head north along the treeline with the corvids in hot pursuit. A Bald Eagle soon joined the circling mob scene, allowing for another amazing direct size comparison with the Steller’s, who appeared a third as big again. Eventually the sea-eagle broke off from its pursuers and continued north, disappearing behind the trees. As far as I know, there were no confirmed reports for the rest of the day

URN:CornellLabOfOrnithology:EBIRD:OBS1319374558	2022-01-20 21:59:52.258041	7973	species	avibase-C481FB24	Steller's Sea-Eagle	Haliaeetus pelagicus				1					United States	US	Maine	US-ME	Lincoln	US-ME-015					Boothbay Harbor Yacht Club	L17437703	P	43.8475516	-69.6454013	2022-01-15	07:55:00	obsr16990	S100811676	Traveling	P22	EBIRD	234	0.274		3	1	G7720688	1	1	1		Brutally cold. Clear (visibility 10 miles); wind variable at 7 mph to NW at 10 mph, gusting to 22 mph; 2º-1 F. Snow cover: the ground is mostly bare, except for scattered patches of ice. West Harbor Pond is completely frozen over, but the water below the bridge in Boothbay Harbor remains open.--We raced over to the Boothbay Harbor Yacht Club, stopped in their parking lot, and raced west across the road, where the sea eagle was quite obvious. A large crowd quickly assembled; unexpectedly, but not really surprisingly, Ann Mitchell was there too. For the first few minutes we were content to view from the initial location, but, realizing that a side view might be better (even if slightly more distant), we slipped down to the bridge across the mouth of West Harbor Pond. Only a few other observers were there when we arrived, but many others later joined us, some parking on the side of the road, or even on the bridge. Not surprisingly, this became an issue in short order, and after a couple of hours a few police arrived to try to keep both cars and observers in line. (It ws impressive to see, though, how quickly the crowds cleared after the eagle left.) We really wanted to see the bird in flight, but it persisted in giving us absolutely crippling views of it perched, for hour after hour; so periodically we'd take our chances and go sit in the car for a while to warm up, before heading back out for another stint for forced sea eagle viewing. --Saturday. Civil twilight begins 06:37, sunrise 07:09; sunset 16:26, civil twilight ends 16:58. Eastern Standard Time.--Mammals: none observed.	continuing mega rarity. extraordinarily cooperative, almost too much so. when we screamed into the parking lot here and got out of the car, it was perched in full sun in a tall pine on the west side of West Harbor Pond, across from the yacht club, and not 100 yards away. the massive eagle was facing us, which allowed for great view of the overall bulk, the massive orangey yellow bill, the beady yellow iris, the details of the shaggy brown head and breast plumage, and the white, graduated tail; but could see only a small piece of the white shoulders (and of only one shoulder to boot). after a while, then, we drifted down to the bridge below, where we could view it from more of an angle, and get a somewhat better appreciation of it. the eagle was quite aware of its surroundings, looking around constantly, but otherwise not doing much: it was big news if it do much as pulled one foot up and tucked it into the plumage. every hour or so it would act as if it might fly: pooping and wiggling the tail; or leaning forward and raising the tail (again pooping?), in each case leading to speculation that it was about to fly. and we all wanted it to fly, we wanted to see the majesty of those broad white shoulders and get a better sense of the overall heft of the bird. despite the apparent flight intention actions, and despite that fact that we were slowly dying in the brutal cold, however, the eagle stubbornly would not take flight. rarely if ever have I waited so long or suffered so much to see a rare bird in flight. after almost four hours, it finally did something dramatic, and turned around on its perch. this presented a possibility that we had not even counted upon: that it might take flight, but instead of flying right past or even over us, it might fly directly away. and that's about what it did: it soon took flight, heading away, and would have been lost behind the trees had it not angled off to the north and up a bit, so we had a decent view of it flight, albeit trending in the wrong direction. just at the end, it turned west again, and we lost it behind trees. as best that we can determine, it was not seen again for the rest of the day. many photos by Jay, Shawn (but none calendar worthy?), and many others.

Super mega-rarity found late yesterday afternoon and alerted to Maine birding community by Nick Lund of Maine Audubon. Many observers on site this morning since daybreak.  I arrived just after 11 am. I watched this massive bird (larger than Bald Eagle) on deep wingbeats cross the skyline about 300 yards offshore, flying from one island to Crow Island. By the time I had arrived to Five Island pier the Steller's Sea Eagle had flown out to Mink Island and fortunately was sitting in a tall tree on the eastern side.  Marshall Iliff showed me my first view of this rare bird from Eurasia. I followed up with the best images I could get from this far away. It's large size is the first indication that  this is a large Sea Eagle. They are often referred to as the world’s most magnificent bird of prey, I watched as the bird flew to the small islands off of Reid State Park where it was later found. The large wings seemed to be followed by the outer primary feathers on the upward and downward strokes. The Steller’s sea-eagle is dark brown, impressive when compared to our native Bald Eagle and it is the largest of all sea-eagles.  It also is the heaviest known eagle. Its large size and ability to fly amazing distances. It is a beautiful Eagle to see. We also had comparisons in size to a Common Raven. There were a couple hundred people on and around the pier. For a moment in time, Georgetown was the epicenter of the Maine birding community, the center of the birding worlds gaze and amazement that his bird is here in Maine. I said about two weeks ago that this bird was coming to Maine. We have everything this bird needs to survive. Food and abundance of many pray options.

URN:CornellLabOfOrnithology:EBIRD:OBS1307181128	2022-01-01 00:03:05.531697	7973	species	avibase-C481FB24	Steller's Sea-Eagle	Haliaeetus pelagicus				1					United States	US	Maine	US-ME	Sagadahoc	US-ME-023					Five Islands	L4294358	H	43.8238825	-69.7095300	2021-12-31	12:06:00	obsr34603	S99795095	Traveling	P22	EBIRD	89	1.061		150	1		1	1	1		Amazing return visit with the sea-eagle, this time with Rosie Faunce joining me.	***mega; adult found yesterday after its stay in Massachusetts 12-20 Dec, and found yesterday in Georgetown, Maine. When Rosie Faunce and I arrived the bird had gone missing for the last 30 min but was apparently believed to be sitting behind Crow Island. We found Todd Day and chatted for just 2-3 min before a call went up that it was flying. I got a quick binoc look and then handed my bins off to Rosie, who was shorter than the crowd and could just see it in windows between heads. Since ID driven her all the way there, I immediately hoisted her up above the crowd height to see the bird and she got on it. Then we had to scurry around the wharf for a second view of it flying S past Malden and Hen Islands and then pulling up to land (obscured) on Mink Island—the southernmost in the Five Islands group. We tried to respot it from the lower wharf area for 10-15 minutes to no avail, connecting with Louis Bevier in the interim. ----While introducing Louis and Todd, some cries flew over the island and Todd noticed them swooping on something which we deduced would likely be the sea-eagle. We could see that whichever eagle it was, was perched just barely out of view so we grabbed the scope and walked up the road a bit, gaining elevation. I stopped at what felt like the right window for a different view and immediately saw it hunkered down low in a notch in the trees. It was not obvious, but the upper third of its body—including its huge bill—could be seen. We got a couple dozen people on it before it shifted perches. It took us a few minutes to realize, but that perch shift seemingly put it in full view from the wharf below, so we scurried back down and Todd quickly spotted it against the skyline of the island in full view. As had happened in Dighton, the bird was surprisingly hard for me to spot, takin a few seconds even in full view in a binocular. The sky was whitish gray behind the bird and I realized that it was because the huge white shoulder patch perfectly broke up the bird’s outline, making it seem like a craggy spruce tip more than the world’s largest eagle. Anyway, these views were excellent and prolonged: we left at 1:30 after 40 minutes of great perched views only to hear it flew S to Reid SB about 10 minutes later. ----Another amazing experience with this bird. Flight photos I have seen confirm the expected: this is the same bird seen in Massachusetts and Atlantic Canada, with the strongly notched p6 and white armpit spots being useful individual identifiers. To my knowledge everyone who wanted to see the bird today (and put in the time it deserved) should have been successful, a welcome change from its recent Massachusetts performance that rewarded only the first responders.
