Nice calm water 40 degrees air.	At the dock, the eagle started out at Malden Island at 11:15. We watched as the eagle flew across to Crow Island and called. Here eagles and crows harassed it and it eventually moved to the back center of the island. At this point I got in my kayak and paddled around the north end and saw the bird on the backside. Keeping off shore 40-50 yards the bird was generally not interested in me. I was able to watch the bird for 25 minutes until a pair of Gulls managed to push it from its perch. I followed its path toward Malden Island. I rediscovered the bird in the center of Mink Island with two Bald Eagles. Like the last spot, the bird sat on a high spruce perch and peered at passing birds and into the water. Wonder how much of its own food it gets vs. steals from gulls and Bald Eagles. I paddled back with it still there at 12:45. At about 1:15 after I had loaded up my kayak the bird flew away to the South out toward the tip of the peninsula as I gathered from the shouts and murmurs of the crowd. Ebird allows me to post 10 pictures, so I maxed out being this bird is so rare!

URN:CornellLabOfOrnithology:EBIRD:OBS1307596918	2022-01-10 12:43:48.702856	7973	species	avibase-C481FB24	Steller's Sea-Eagle	Haliaeetus pelagicus				1					United States	US	Maine	US-ME	Sagadahoc	US-ME-023					Five Islands	L4294358	H	43.8238825	-69.7095300	2021-12-31	11:24:00	obsr49257	S99822396	Traveling	P22	EBIRD	134	0.653		150	1		1	1	1			America's Most Famous Eagle, present for (at least) its second day on the islands off the Five Islands pier. Upon arrival (at about 11:20) I quickly learned that the bird had last been seen retreating to the far side of Crow Island after showing well at various spots during much of the morning. The throng of birders who had gathered on the public pier numbered over 100, and behaved remarkably well. This is an active pier, with lobster boats offloading their catch, as well as pulling traps and loading them onto flatbed trailers. Not once did I hear any frustration among the lobstermen/women or the birders. I was joined by Marshall Iliff and his friend Rosie a few minutes after noon. A handy kayaker paddled to the far side of Crow Island, and not long thereafter, the Sea-Eagle was picked up in flight flying south toward Maiden and Hen Islands, not far off the end of our pier. with at least one nearby Bald Eagle. Initially mobbed by gulls, the two eagles flew in steady, powered flight toward Maiden and Hen, going between them. I moved to the western side of the lobster shack, ready to continue to scope the bird if it emerged. It popped back out at the south end of Hen Island, flying toward Mink Island. I tracked the bird in the scope to where I could no longer see it, but felt confident that I saw the flight change to perch somewhere, out of view. The Bald Eagle continue to fly past Mink Island, but the Sea-Eagle was not with it. Marshall and Rosie rejoined me, and Marshall was also confident that he had a good idea where the bird had perched. He examined the area in my scope, but was unable to find the bird. ----At this point, we were joined by Louis Brevier, my first in-person meeting. While talking, I started to notice crows mobbing a single spot near the skyline of Mink Island. At Marshall's suggestion, we started seeking higher ground, marking the spot on the island by a lone White Pine, and with a change of angle, Marshall was able to find the bird through a small window, perched on the far side of the island. About one-quarter or so of the bird was in view, with its massive, yellow bill showing well. We were able to set up two scopes on this line, and a few dozen folks were able to get this partial view. While watching, we saw the bird do a few perched flaps, and it eventually settled down slope of the island. We also noticed that the plucky kayaker was below this area, aiming a camera not far up. We all headed back down to the lobster shack, and started scoping the skyline again. We found the bird in good silhouette, with a wider window of view, and at this point anyone who was interested in seeing the bird could enjoy scope views for at least thirty minutes. ----This bird was difficult to detect against the skyline. The large white patch on the shoulder breaks up a typical silhouette of a raptor, making my raptor-seeking eye miss the bird. ----Large eagle, much more girth than nearby Bald Eagles. Immense yellow bill. Dark brown overall, with thin pale stripes on the head. Large white shoulder patches seen perched. These patches form a giant white leading edge of the interior two-thirds of the wing in flight. Large, white tail. Truly unmistakable. Nothing notably different about the flight style compared to Bald Eagles; perhaps a bit "heavier."

Photos.----Originally found and photographed by Linda Tharp on 30 December 2021. Likely the same bird that had appeared on the Taunton River in Massachusetts a couple weeks ago and has been appearing throughout North America (Alaska, Texas, Nova Scotia) over the past year.----Large eagle perched at top of conifer tree on island to the southeast of the wharf parking lot (appears to be Hen Island). Dark body with contrasting white shoulder patches visible while perched, Bird was initially facing away from the parking lot but periodically turned its head to reveal noticeably large orange bill. Immature Bald Eagle perched 20-30 m on adjacent tree top for comparison (Larger than BAEA). Bird took flight and headed north towards Crow Island, perched briefly on top of a visible confider perch, but then moved to the northeast side of the island, limiting visibility from the wharf parking lot. URN:CornellLabOfOrnithology:EBIRD:OBS1307875269	2022-01-01 22:45:01.238371	7973	species	avibase-C481FB24	Steller's Sea-Eagle	Haliaeetus pelagicus				1					United States	US	Maine	US-ME	Sagadahoc	US-ME-023		14			Golf Cove	L17280730	P	43.8308295	-69.7102852	2021-12-31	08:30:00	obsr1456493	S99860963	Stationary	P21	EBIRD_ME	75			2	0	G7625968	1	1	1			Mega-rarity, incredible day all around. I got to Five Islands at around 7:45, a few dozen birders were milling about on the wharf and seawatching to see if they could spot the bird. Around half an hour later, someone yells, "We got it!" and take of running up the hill from the water. Everyone else grabbed their scopes and ran after him. When someone questioned him about where the bird was sighted from, he responded, "Just get in your car and drive". I got into the car with Nellie and checked my New England Birder's Discord and saw that someone had crossposted the coordinates from the listserv, so we drove about a mile north to the end of Moore's Turnpike. We joined the birders on the shore to see the eagle about half a mile out on Mac Mahan Island, right next to a house on the southern tip. It was incredible even from that far away, the white edge of the wings practically glowed in the morning sun. It eventually flew south to Turnip Island and I got my first good look at the bird through some very kind birders' scopes. The beak and head absurdly proportioned, it looks almost like a cartoon drawing of an eagle. The most striking thing was the eyes, the irises were a cool white. After a while the eagle flew from Turnip Island to the mainland and landed on a tree at the end of Blue Heron lane. The views weren't great, but luckily it decided to fly and land on the southern side of Golf Cove just a couple hundred feet away. Views from the docks were great, it was partially obscured by branches and only half in the sun, but it didn't really matter. Unfortunately, all birders were asked to leave by the property owners at around 9:45 as it was a liability issue. The eagle decided to head out at the same time as the birders, and it wouldn't be long before I got to see it again.

Over the course of the time here, there were probably 250 people, with up to 100-150 at times. Except for the earliest hour or so, I did not carefully record all individuals. Included here are birds encountered scanning the Sheepscot and residential lanes nearby. From 06:50 until about 08:30, I scanned from the end of the public wharf. I then walked to the end of Moore's Turnpike when we got word that the eagle was visible from there. I spent from about 08:45 until 10:30 at this site. I then returned to the public wharf via streets. I also walked to the end of Misty Lane twice, once alone. I was otherwise in the wharf area from 11:00 until 13:50, when I departed for Reid State Park. It was mild for this time of year, and relatively calm. The start was somewhat misty but that cleared before sunrise to partly cloudy skies, which became sunny for mid-morning. The tide was nearing high in the morning, then falling from about 08:30 onward. Temps mid 30s to 40s. [Track erroneous because I did not stop list or edit track]	Amazing luck that this wandering individual was found in Maine after last being seen 20 December along the Taunton River in se. Massachusetts. A local living near the Five Islands wharf had taken photos of the bird and reported this to Nick Lund's blog. Following up, we learned that she, Linda Tharp, had seen the bird on 29 December and then photographed it on 30 December.----A huge eagle that when seen with Bald Eagle appeared one third larger. The bill was especially deep and arched as well as being a rich orangish yellow (both cere and mandibles). Overall a slaty brown with broad white band across upper forewing (lesser and median coverts) and much more narrowly along forewing on underside reaching the wrist. The tail and upper tail coverts were white. The tail was wedge-shaped and sharply tapered to a point; the white on the upper tail coverts encroached on the lower rump (this was noticeable as the bird flew away and quite a distinct difference from Bald Eagle if the white on the wings could not be seen). Another prominent white area was the thighs, flanks, and under tail coverts. With my scope, I was able to see frosted light gray to whitish on the forehead and perhaps some of head. The eye was whitish and rather beady-looking in the big head. The large toes were yellow with black talons.----Characters linking this as the same individual in Massachusetts and before that in Canada were visible in photographs. On the right wing, there is a thin break in the webbing of the inner web on p6. On the right side near the axillars, there is a small white spot. The shape and ragged pattern of the rear border to the white forewing bar also show similar features to flight shots of the bird in Canada.----This morning, Matthew Gilbert and Reed Robinson (and ??) located the bird from a private property vantage north of the wharf (at the end Moore's Turnpike, N 43.83046 W 69.70974). The eagle was perched atop a conifer on the southeast corner of MacMahan Island (perch here N 43.83833 W 69.70564) that was 0.92 kilometers away (over 1/2 mile). With clear and sunny skies, the bird was easily identifiable. The eagle then flew to a perch on Turnip Island, only 0.45 km (a bit over 1/4 mile). We then watched the eagle fly out over the Sheepscot River and at one point seemingly attempt to grab a fish. It was almost as far out as the Bull Ledge buoy (green can) about 1 km away. This is a shallow ledge submerged at high tide, as it was at this time; so perhaps a good spot to find fish. This bird was easily identified at that distance. Gulls harassed the eagle, which eventually flew into trees at the end of Misty Lane and the other side of Gotts Cove (erroneously labeled "Golf Cove" on Google maps). At this point, the bird was 100 to 150 meters away but not easily seen in the trees where it perched. By 10:30, we were asked politely to leave the private property. Returning to the public wharf, we saw the eagle perch on Crow Island (at one point arching its neck and pointing its beak skyward as it called, a strange deep barking sound). We also saw the bird perch at the tops of trees on Malden Island and later Mink Island. At 13:40, the eagle flew south, more or less directly. I followed it as speck in the distance, and we assumed the bird might be off Reid State Park. No sooner had we concluded that when at 13:48 I got a text from Lance Benner that the bird had landed on the islands to the south off Griffith Head. It is possible that we could see the bird as it neared those ledges, which are 7 km (4.37 miles) from the wharf. For a good portion of this flight, I could still make out occasionally the white forewings on the upstroke and/or discern the point of white up the rump.

How does this species exist, let alone this individual make it's way to the northeast? massive bill, bulky chest and back, peaked head, bulging secondaries with exceedingly long primaries, and wedge shaped tail giving oscillating impression of power and awkwardness.----Initially relocated this morning from a private road near five islands boat landing, where evicted masses waited for it to emerge. It eventually did so after about 20 minutes of waiting, flying out of Golf Cove, out over Crow Island (flushing a seemingly miniature bald eagle en route), and landing on Malden Island where it sat regally for the next half hour or so, giving staggering views for all. It then flew over to Crow Island where it landed out of view. Liftoff from Malden towards crow especially dramatic, with the massive bird seeming to fly in slow motion through the still air and over still water, with a calling loon setting the soundtrack. We watched this from a rock outcrop exposed by low tide, away from the mob and making for a truly magical experience.

URN:CornellLabOfOrnithology:EBIRD:OBS1306871539	2022-01-19 22:25:15.67574	7973	species	avibase-C481FB24	Steller's Sea-Eagle	Haliaeetus pelagicus				1					United States	US	Maine	US-ME	Sagadahoc	US-ME-023					Five Islands	L4294358	H	43.8238825	-69.7095300	2021-12-31	08:45:00	obsr513087	S99770491	Stationary	P21	EBIRD	300			1	1		1	1	1		NOTE: This list includes our first stop at Moore's Turnpike where MG refound the bird. We were asked to leave (this is a private road) and relocated to Five Islands where the STSE turned up shortly after.----John Berry picked me up in Topsham and we drove down together. John departed part way through my stay at Five Islands.----A HUGE crowd of easily 200+ birds and photographers gathered on the wharf (with working lobsterman) and adjacent lawn/rocks. Practically everyone I know was here, between Five Islands and Moore's Turnpike: Doug Hitchcox, Louis Bevier, Noah Gibb, Turk Duddy, Nick Lund, Luke Seitz, Reed Robinson, Matthew Gilbert, Chris Sayers, Glenn and Anna Hodgkins, Doug Suitor, Jan Pierson, Ilsa and Ben Tucker, Gary Jarvis and Christine Murray, Tom and Jessy Foley, Laurie Pocher, Sean Hatch, Marian Zimmerman, Michael Boardman, Ethan and Ingrid Whitaker (mega congrats on the big year Ethan!!), Stan and Joan Deorsey, Susan Fenn, Charles Duncan and Laura Blutstein, and certainly others that I'm forgetting.----Cries of joy, laughter, and clapping accompanied each appearance of the eagle and many congratulations and slaps on the back were exchanged. The air was buzzing with excitement.----Absolutely epic bird and it's all the better that it's in my home county :-)	****MEGA****1st state record. The saga continues...----This bird was photographed yesterday afternoon by Linda Tharp near Five Islands and reported to Nick Lund, who put the word out. Matthew Gilbert refound the bird around 8:20-30 from Moore's Turnpike co-op (private property).----The bird eventually moved over to Five Islands where perhaps a couple hundred people viewed it. Between Moore's Turnpike and Five Islands, the bird moved to and from at least eight different perches while I was present, sitting in one spot for extended periods of time and occasionally disappearing on the back side of an island for a while. It took off just as I was leaving and flew south to Reid SP, where it was still being seen at the time of this writing (3:50 p.m.).----This was an absolutely enormous eagle, easily exceeding the size of the numerous Bald Eagles that were usually nearby and occasionally harassing the Steller's. --The MASSIVE bright yellow bill was very easily seen at most times and positively glowed in the sunlight. When perched, a large and conspicuous white slash was prominent on each wing and the bird had the appearance of wearing a pair of fluffy white "pants". The tail was white (perhaps a bit dingy) and the body plumage was a very dark brown with some golden streaking down the nape. A pale whitish eye was occasionally visible.----In flight the Steller's had a different profile than a Bald Eagle, looking bulkier but somehow also lankier with more planklike wings and a more protruding and sagging head and neck (no doubt this appearance was at least partially due to the massive bill, but the neck did appear longer and the head smaller in proportion to the body). The white in the wings appearing as a large slash when perched proved to be the marginal and maybe lesser(?) coverts and perhaps into the alula and primary coverts, covering the entire leading edge of the wing (except the primaries) with white on both the upper and lower surfaces, but being more extensive in the upper.----The bird called on one occasion upon alighting atop a conifer on Crow Island. It gave a series of repetitive, low barking notes with a somewhat hoarse sound to it, unlike the clear-toned calls that observers in Massachusetts recorded on 12-20-21.----[notes written from my memory of watching this bird through my scope]----Photos shortly

URN:CornellLabOfOrnithology:EBIRD:OBS1308016042	2022-01-01 22:45:01.238371	7973	species	avibase-C481FB24	Steller's Sea-Eagle	Haliaeetus pelagicus				1					United States	US	Maine	US-ME	Sagadahoc	US-ME-023		14			Golf Cove	L17280730	P	43.8308295	-69.7102852	2021-12-31	08:30:00	obsr2096184	S99874928	Stationary	P21	EBIRD_ME	75			2	0	G7625968	1	1	1			Mega-rarity, incredible day all around. I got Five Islands at around 7:45, a few dozen birders were milling about on the wharf and seawatching to see if they could spot the bird. Around half an hour later, someone yells, "We got it!" and take of running up the hill from the water. Everyone else grabbed their scopes and ran after him. When someone questioned him about where the bird was sighted from, he responded, "Just get in your car and drive". I got into the car with Nellie and checked my New England Birder's Discord and saw that someone had crossposted the coordinates from the listserv, so we drove about a mile north to the end of Moore's Turnpike. We joined the birders on the shore to see the eagle about half a mile out on Mac Mahan Island, right next to a house on the southern tip. It was incredible even from that far away, the white edge of the wings practically glowed in the morning sun. It eventually flew south to Turnip Island and I got my first good look at the bird through some very kind birders' scopes. The beak and head absurdly proportioned, it looks almost like a cartoon drawing of an eagle. The most striking thing was the eyes, the irises were a cool white. After a while the eagle flew from Turnip Island to the mainland and landed on a tree at the end of Blue Heron lane. The views weren't great, but luckily it decided to fly and land on the southern side of Golf Cove just a couple hundred feet away. Views from the docks were great, it was partially obscured by branches and only half in the sun, but it didn't really matter. Unfortunately, all birders were asked to leave by the property owners at around 9:45 as it was a liability issue. The eagle decided to head out at the same time as the birders, and it wouldn't be long before I got to see it again.

Many thanks to the other birders sharing scope views. ----I missed this closer to home, and had spent most of my holiday break checking the Mass Rare Bird Alerts thread, much to the annoyance of my wife. However, on the morning of the 31st, when I told her it was still being seen, she suggested I go. Many thanks to her for tolerating my day long-absence. I got a late start leaving Cambridge--a few minutes before 11AM. About 30 minutes into my drive I saw a message pop up that the eagle had flown, I considered the alternative of spending the day on Plum Island instead, but decided I would rather drive to Maine and miss the bird than cancel and miss the bird. With light traffic and only moderate speeding (which may have turned out to be crucial for the satisfying looks I got at Five Islands), I made it in a bit over 2.5 hours. I parked in one of the first spots I found about 0.2 miles down the road from the wharf, got my things together, and jogged down to the dock, arriving at about 1:30 PM. My timing was perfect, other birders graciously shared scope views. The bird flew at 1:38.

Spent the first two hours scoping from the end of the pier. The bird was spotted around 8:30 am on small island (to the left of Crow Island). URN:CornellLabOfOrnithology:EBIRD:OBS1307569160	2022-01-01 17:34:12.745018	7973	species	avibase-C481FB24	Steller's Sea-Eagle	Haliaeetus pelagicus				1					United States	US	Maine	US-ME	Sagadahoc	US-ME-023					Five Islands	L4294358	H	43.8238825	-69.7095300	2022-01-01	06:47:00	obsr489493	S99828750	Stationary	P21	EBIRD_ME	197			1	1		1	1	1			Arrived a little before 7 and spent the first 2ish hours scanning surrounding area from the end of the pier. Someone alerted the group that they had the bird in their scope, but it was distant and only visible from one vantage point on the pier. Not too longer after, the bird flew out of sight and the crowd started to work their way off the pier to give the working fishermen room. I walked up onto a rock on the edge of the parking lot and started scanning the islands again and quickly caught a flash of white at the top of a conifer on Crow Island: the Steller's Sea-Eagle! What a distinctive bird, hard to mistake it when compared to any other expected species in this area. In the lighting, the bird appeared mostly black with clean white patches on the wings, white "pants" and a ridiculously large orange bill. White tail seen in flight. The bird was absolutely stunning in flight. Awesome experience, despite the fog and rain.

URN:CornellLabOfOrnithology:EBIRD:OBS1310124109	2022-01-04 11:40:19.656732	7973	species	avibase-C481FB24	Steller's Sea-Eagle	Haliaeetus pelagicus				1					United States	US	Maine	US-ME	Sagadahoc	US-ME-023		14			Grey Havens Inn, 96 Seguinland Rd	L17317043	P	43.8109951	-69.7209549	2022-01-02	14:30:00	obsr294200	S100057816	Stationary	P21	EBIRD	60			1	1		0	1	1			I arrived at Grey Havens Inn about one minute after the Steller’s Sea-Eagle departed!  I was told that the eagle had been perched near the top of the same tree on Wood Island for the previous 3 ½ hours.  Another birder told me that the Steller’s Sea-Eagle flew over my head chasing a Bald Eagle that had a fish. If I had only thought to look upward, I would have seen it!  That was the frustrating beginning to what was eventually a wonderful and exhilarating experience.  The one hundred or so birders that were at Grey Havens Inn gradually departed.  Several birders suggested that I remain at Grey Havens Inn, since their best guess is that the eagle could return to Wood Island since it had spent so much of the day there. For the first few minutes, I was alone looking for the eagle.  Then other birders gradually trickled in.  After about 30 minutes of waiting, one birder called out “I think I might see a Bald Eagle”. Disappointed, but hoping that perhaps the bird was not a Bald Eagle but a Steller’s Sea-Eagle, I quickly got my binoculars on the bird.  Extensive white shoulders, large white wedge-shaped tail, white leg feathers, with a fish in its massive orange bill.  I yelled out “it’s the Steller’s Sea-Eagle”.  An enormous relief followed by unbridled joy.  The Steller’s Sea-Eagle landed in the top of a tree on the mainland, perhaps 100 or 200m north of the tree at which it had spent the previous few hours before I arrived. The number of birders present continued to increase, perhaps reaching about 100 people, and each person quickly got onto the bird. It was wonderful to see so many happy, smiling birders.  The Steller’s Sea-Eagle remained atop the same tree, sometimes preening, looking around, appearing a little restless, as if it was about to leave. After about 30 minutes, the Steller’s Sea-Eagle left the tree flying to the northwest and then out of sight.  A truly amazing and incredible experience. Certainly, one of the most thrilling and memorable birds I have ever seen.

URN:CornellLabOfOrnithology:EBIRD:OBS1308109324	2022-05-09 11:54:44.073117	7973	species	avibase-C481FB24	Steller's Sea-Eagle	Haliaeetus pelagicus				1					United States	US	Maine	US-ME	Sagadahoc	US-ME-023		14			Grey Havens Inn	L17286403	P	43.8109420	-69.7204540	2022-01-01	14:18:00	obsr154922	S99883534	Traveling	P22	EBIRD	59	0.201		1	1		0	1	1			I mean, where do I begin? This bird is so unbelievable it deserves the full story. I knew about this bird’s North American wanderings for some time, but it seemed too distant or fleeting to pursue. But yesterday when I saw that it was continuing to hang out here, with wide open flights and cheap rental cars, I decided to make the trip. This morning I saw word that it had been refound in the same area as before, off Five Islands. I boarded my flight and hoped it would stick, hoping that the poor weather would keep it down. I arrived and booked it up 295. When I got to Five Islands the wharf was packed. At least 50 birders in line for the lobsterman’s boat trips to Mink Island, where the bird was apparently staked out, out of sight from land, and just as many lined up scoping the distant island. With not enough daylight left to wait in the line, I resigned myself to waiting for a distant look if the bird ever took off from its hiding place. As the boat set out again below us, the crowd noticed that it didn’t round the tip of the distant island like before, but instead hugged the shoreline. I began to realize that the bird had flown and somehow escaped our notice! Right then I got a text that there were good views from the inn, and sped over. When I arrived the eagle was perched in a spruce at the far southern end of Wood Island, across the harbor from the inn. Scope views revealed the massive orange-yellow bill, and chocolate brown color, and white tail. The bird remained perched for nearly an hour, occasionally moving a bit, and revealing white shoulder patches. I resolved to just stand in the rain and fog and wait for it to fly. It briefly vocalized twice—a raspy, gull-like call, not at all like its congener, Bald Eagle. After nearly an hour I was chatting with other birders when I heard a commotion, and looked up to see a huge bird flying toward us. At first I thought it was a heron. Then I got bins on it and realized it was the eagle! It flew past us at about 50 yards. It was an absolute behemoth. It flew effortlessly and boldly. Completely regal and a bird that is clearly used to being the ruler of its domain. As it flew it was harassed by crows. And just like that, it was gone. An unbelievable experience. Abysmal but diagnostic pics pending.

After failed attempt in Mass, I needed to make another attempt for this bird.  Left work on New Years day at 1030pm.  7 hours of driving and a caffeine pill later I arrived on no sleep (attempted to, but not successful).  We staked out the docks, but around 830am got the message the bird was being seen on nearby Wood Island.  Luckily was one of the prime spots for parking which made for an easy escape and was one of the first to arrive at the Grey Havens Inn (gracious owners were allowing birders to utilize their place for viewing).  Bird was perched atop trees a few hundred yards out into the river.  Shifted once to the opposite end of the island, eventually taking off North and across the channel.  With already so much time committed to this trip, I made the call to head home getting back at 430pm.  14 hours of driving, 2 hours of birding, and 1 spectacular bird!	Spectacular.  Re-found by other birders on Wood Island while the majority of us were at the docks.  Mad chase to get out and get to the spot.  Observed the bird for approximately 30 minutes before it took off across the river.  Believe it is estimated that approximately 4000 birds remain, most on the kamchatka peninsula in Russia.  Size was deceiving until the bird took flight, and when it did, man was it impressive.  Bird averages about 50% heavier than our Bald Eagle which was very evident during flight with an almost pot belly appearance.  Striking white markings with a very large, bright yellow beak.  This was a memorable chase that I will not soon forget.

Spent roughly first two hours scoping from the end of the pier. The bird was spotted around 8:30/9 am on small island (to the left of Crow Island). It was only visible for a few minutes, from the far corner of the pier until it flew out of sight. It perched in another conifer for about 20 minutes before flying to the next small island on the right. We left it perched, still visible but more obscured at this point. Enormous and very distinctive black and white raptor with white wing patches, white wedge-shaped tail, and comically large orange-yellow bill.

Shortly after 8:30 am bird glimpsed from Five Islands Lobster Co wharf on small island left of Crow Island. Shortly thereafter, bird flew and perched for 1/2 hour or so on tree on Crow Island. Bird flew right to next island and perched for several minutes before continuing to fly further right and farther down coast, perching from time to time. Large eagle with large orange bill, orange feet, white tail and shoulder patches.
